<div id="review_215881749" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/215881749" />
    <a title="Sam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/72837-sam"><img alt="Sam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1178153459p2/72837.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/215881749?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam" href="/user/show/72837-sam">Sam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140442519" href="/book/show/576781.Lost_Illusions">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer215881749" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1620982377281136401">As a young man from the provinces, I do so enjoy a "young man from the provinces" novel - that being said, this might be the mother of all such works, primarily because of Balzac's unrelenting attention to detail. Like many a novelist who pretends to traffic in moral opposition (literature good, journalism bad!) Balzac saves his juiciest and most loving description for the baddies - the description of the pestilent Wooden Galleries where literary fortunes are bought and sold in shanty-town books</span>
  <span id="freeText1620982377281136401" style="display:none">As a young man from the provinces, I do so enjoy a "young man from the provinces" novel - that being said, this might be the mother of all such works, primarily because of Balzac's unrelenting attention to detail. Like many a novelist who pretends to traffic in moral opposition (literature good, journalism bad!) Balzac saves his juiciest and most loving description for the baddies - the description of the pestilent Wooden Galleries where literary fortunes are bought and sold in shanty-town booksellers is enough to convince anyone that the corrupt world is simply more fun than any idealist paradise could ever be. <br />Also worth noticing is the panoply of voices at play here, from literary parody to poetry to newspaper copy. If Bakhtin is right, and what we're looking for in a novel is modes of discourse wreaking havoc with each other, then Lost Illusions might very well be the perfect example: check out Lucien's review of a Paris play in which the play is not mentioned as a pitch-perfect parody of hack journalism, and let me know afterward if you don't think Balzac is a wizard.<br />That being said, there are a few drawbacks here. The salon scenes here show how much Proust was influenced by Balzac in his jaundiced eye for the nobility, but a nagging misogyny cramps Balzac's style and makes the women seem like ineffectual nincompoops; compare this to Proust's majestic evocation of Madame Swan at the end of In the Shadow of Young Girls in Flower and you'll see how much 60 years of societal development and being homosexual can do for a novelist's understanding of gender roles.<br />Also, the moral arc at the end is a little hampered by a sudden denouement - which I won't spoil. This means we don't get the kind of bitter irony you might receive at the hands of a Flaubert, and I, for one, am into that sort of thing. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1620982377281136401" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3362858290" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3362858290" />
    <a title="Steve R" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51602729-steve-r"><img alt="Steve R" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1550608435p2/51602729.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3362858290?book_show_action=true">Jun 07, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve R" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve R" href="/user/show/51602729-steve-r">Steve R</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3362858290" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6835955586294650581">As one of Balzac‚Äôs longer novels, this one exemplifies all of his outstanding strengths: a marvelous ability to delineate characters of variant physical, emotional, intellectual and above all moral natures (just one example: a nefarious lawyer is described as a torpid viper with magpie eyes); his astounding skill in weaving plots so complex yet still understandable, often involving minute financial details and always conflicting elements of personal vanity and ambition; and finally, his setting </span>
  <span id="freeText6835955586294650581" style="display:none">As one of Balzac‚Äôs longer novels, this one exemplifies all of his outstanding strengths: a marvelous ability to delineate characters of variant physical, emotional, intellectual and above all moral natures (just one example: a nefarious lawyer is described as a torpid viper with magpie eyes); his astounding skill in weaving plots so complex yet still understandable, often involving minute financial details and always conflicting elements of personal vanity and ambition; and finally, his setting up of good and bad forces of altruistic idealism and selfish egoism, putting them in play against one another, and leaving the reader hoping against hope that the good guys will win. After the dozen or so of Balzac‚Äôs works I‚Äôve read, I‚Äôve come to always expect an eventual disappointment of failed dreams: he seems to have a predisposition for seeing the naivety of those who innocently expect the best of their fellow men as an inevitable weakness allowing the cynical, self-serving machinations of the greedy to defeat them.<br /><br />Set in both the capital of Paris and a remote province of France, the novel is replete with descriptions of the various social and political forces at play during the Restoration period following the Napoleonic wars. The nose-in-the-air aristocrats are there, with their staunch religiosity and fervent belief in the monarchy. The grasping bourgeoisie appear, largely represented by the generally despised professions of journalism, book publishing, money lending and law. It would appear Balzac himself had poor experiences both in reading his daily newspaper and in dealing with publishers, as there is little to admire in his portrayal of the practitioners of these trades. The supposed social class opponents are quite similarly portrayed: characters of both upper and middle classes are vainglorious, jealous, disloyal, conniving, and always looking for the edge that will put them ahead of their peers.<br /><br />The childlike naivety of Lucien and the blinkered determination of the printer-turned-inventor David provide the basis of ‚ÄòTwo Poets‚Äô, the first of the novel‚Äôs three sections. The precipitous ups and downs of fortune for Lucien in Paris form the basis of ‚ÄòA Provincial in Paris‚Äô, the second part; while the culmination of the fates of David and his loving wife, Eve, who has more brains and awareness of the forces arrayed against them than both her husband and his friend put together, forms ‚ÄòEve and David‚Äô, the final part. It is a masterfully complex, highly dramatic and quite suspenseful story during which I found myself metaphorically booing the bad guys, yelling at the stupidity of their victims, and cheering on the noble efforts of those who strove to save them from their fate. The culmination is, though somewhat blunted, well worth the time it takes to read the roughly 600pp. which it takes one to get there.<br /><br />Part way through the work, in an almost offhand comment, Balzac refers to ‚Äòthe bourgeois taste for literature without ideas‚Äô. This got me wondering if he had inadvertently shown his true colours. For such a master of characterization, of social scene painting and even of psychological analysis, there is a relative shallowness to all the themes presented. Really, other than the fact that some people are not to be trusted and are largely out to take advantage of others for their own selfish ends, I found little depth to the moral arguments presented. Even the Abbe‚Äôs Machiavellian perorations to Lucien which thematically bring the novel to a close seem to present quite a simplistic outlook: ‚ÄòWe are all slaves to something, either a failing or a necessity and must proceed by stealth against others to achieve our desires. Society has usurped so many rights over the individual that he is compelled to act in self defense.‚Äô Even assuming that these ‚Äòrights‚Äô had ever really existed, I would say that this ‚Äòdo it to them before they do it to you‚Äô philosophy is one that someone with such understanding of the workings of men and women in his society could have improved upon.<br /><br />Still, the novel is extremely engaging and exceptionally well crafted. It is just a shame that such fine brush work could not have resulted in a more moving and insightful canvas.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6835955586294650581" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12868601030404292458">Having finished A Harlot High and Low I was keen to read the book that set the scene Lost Illusions, it did not disappoint. Lucien Chardon the main character is poor but his impoverished mother is of aristocratic descent. He lives in the provinces in Angoul√™me. He is poor, completely spoilt, lazy, impatient, handsome and wants to get ahead. His widowed mother, sister √àve and his best friend, David S√©chard all spoil him and support his high opinion of his talents as a poet and writer. <br/><br/>The book is</span>
  <span id="freeText12868601030404292458" style="display:none">Having finished A Harlot High and Low I was keen to read the book that set the scene Lost Illusions, it did not disappoint. Lucien Chardon the main character is poor but his impoverished mother is of aristocratic descent. He lives in the provinces in Angoul√™me. He is poor, completely spoilt, lazy, impatient, handsome and wants to get ahead. His widowed mother, sister √àve and his best friend, David S√©chard all spoil him and support his high opinion of his talents as a poet and writer. <br /><br />The book is divided into three parts. His rise in the town and support by Mme de Bargeton a leading figure in society. In Paris she realises that Lucien is lowly born and takes appropriate action. The second part of the book is Lucien‚Äôs time in Paris and his rise. However, he neglects his poetry and book writing to become a journalist orca better term would be a blackmail. He has the chance to take the high road but chooses the easy, debauched life where he gets his just desserts. <br /><br />The third part of the book is his return from Pariscin disgrace. The story also focuses on David and Eve who are being forced to give up a paper making invention. Lucien tries to help with hom on a riverbank meeting the villain Vautrin who persuades him to go to Paris. <br /><br />Balzac obviously hated journalism and duplicity which are key themes in the novel. The difference between the province‚Äôs and Paris are also highlighted in terms of clothes, culture and the law. Balzac also is realistic with what happens with the crooks and villains which is refreshing. Well worth a read to see France between 1837-1843. </span>

<div id="review_111853698" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/111853698" />
    <a title="AC" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1655437-ac"><img alt="AC" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1228702469p2/1655437.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/111853698?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="AC" class="user" itemprop="url" name="AC" href="/user/show/1655437-ac">AC</a>
      </span>

        rated it

<div id="review_2234145069" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2234145069" />
    <a title="Jack" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16159568-jack"><img alt="Jack" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1463504215p2/16159568.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2234145069?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jack" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jack" href="/user/show/16159568-jack">Jack</a>

        rated it

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16159568-jack?shelf=19th-century">19th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16159568-jack?shelf=europe">europe</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2234145069" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10697297878088824955">This isn't so much a <i>Bildungsroman</i> as it is an exploration of a young man's failure to mature. Balzac and Dickens are two sides of a coin - Dickens is 'the English Balzac' and Balzac 'the French Dickens'. Of Dickens I've only read <i>The Pickwick Papers</i>, a gentle comedy, and <i>Hard Times</i>, one of his minor works, so I can't be sure whose side to declaim, but Balzac certainly seems more hard-hearted. Since I'm not fluent in French, I presume his characters' names are less silly, at least.<br/><br/>This has the m</span>
  <span id="freeText10697297878088824955" style="display:none">This isn't so much a <i>Bildungsroman</i> as it is an exploration of a young man's failure to mature. Balzac and Dickens are two sides of a coin - Dickens is 'the English Balzac' and Balzac 'the French Dickens'. Of Dickens I've only read <i>The Pickwick Papers</i>, a gentle comedy, and <i>Hard Times</i>, one of his minor works, so I can't be sure whose side to declaim, but Balzac certainly seems more hard-hearted. Since I'm not fluent in French, I presume his characters' names are less silly, at least.<br /><br />This has the makings of a five-star work brought down by the conceit of the <i>La Com√©die humaine</i> - the novel doesn't really end. I'm interested in reading the other works that relate to this one, but if Balzac wasn't explicitly intending to write an interwoven tapestry of French life, he might've given the book a better ending! The sequel hook for <i>A Harlot High and Low</i> is a little hokey, though I'm sure there's a good novel behind it. <br /><br />That aside, <i>Lost Illusions</i> is incredible, a complete blend of plotty readability with subtle flashes of literary insight. (Well, it's not always subtle...) Anyone with a shred of ambition in their bodies will sympathize with Lucien's struggle to win renown to some degree. Having read this, <i>Madame Bovary</i> and <i>Les Miserables</i> I've gained a kind of distanced fascination with French history of the period. I don't understand the political landscape of Liberals and Royalists and people-who-still-like-Napoleon, and I don't know how much sou make a franc, or how much a thousand francs are worth. I'm sure Balzac's contemporary audience didn't share this distanced reading, and knowledge of history would certainly enrich reading literature of the time, but - since Lucien is essentially an ignorant to the murky loyalties of politics and has no idea how to save his money, I find myself more sympathetic to his struggle in our shared ignorance. A new flavour emerges from the text. <br /><br />That ignorance probably won't lead me to re-read this any time soon though. The second act of the book in Paris is spellbinding throughout, but the business squabbles of the final act go on a little too long for the reader that can't keep track of the mounting thousands of francs in constant exchange. <br /><br />If one is interested in reading Balzac, I can say in my enjoyment of this that it is as good a place to start as any. I'm in awe of Balzac's famous work ethic, and might hope merely in reading him to absorb some of that sensibility into myself. My future is set: more Balzac, more coffee, and hopefully more writing!</span>
  <a data-text-id="10697297878088824955" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_326567228" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/326567228" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6971227-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564001692p2/6971227.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/326567228?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/6971227-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781593083151" href="/book/show/702443.Lost_Illusions">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6971227-david?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer326567228" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6802106587026836986">Illusions! Lost ones! Where are they? Joking about it now, 'lost illusions' is a really sad thought, you can never get them back! The notion of illusion in fiction is something really interesting to me, and I think I dwell on it quite a bit in my reviews either consciously or unconsciously. I mean, is there anyone really without illusions? I hope not, it seems like an awfully sad life to live without illusions. Whenever I think of illusionment or disillusionment, my mind always floats away to Wa</span>
  <span id="freeText6802106587026836986" style="display:none">Illusions! Lost ones! Where are they? Joking about it now, 'lost illusions' is a really sad thought, you can never get them back! The notion of illusion in fiction is something really interesting to me, and I think I dwell on it quite a bit in my reviews either consciously or unconsciously. I mean, is there anyone really without illusions? I hope not, it seems like an awfully sad life to live without illusions. Whenever I think of illusionment or disillusionment, my mind always floats away to Wallace Steven's poem "Disillusionment at Ten o'Clock." The poem mourns the loss of illusion, or imagination, of the modern world, as a result of the rise of routine and superficiality. He mourns:<blockquote>
  <i> The houses are haunted<br /> By white night-gowns.<br />...People are not going<br /> To dream of baboons and periwinkles.</i>
</blockquote>The world, to Stevens, has become unimaginative, "haunted" by ghosts without imagination of creativity. People don't have imagination to dream of silly things, they are burdened by the dullness of reality, the focus on the real value of things, appearances. Childhood dreams of baboons and periwinkles, gaudy playfulness and uniqueness of dress are forgone for the seriousness of the world, a deliberate but deadening disillusionment. <i>
  <blockquote><br /> Only, here and there, an old sailor,<br /> Drunk and asleep in his boots,<br /> Catches tigers<br /> In red weather.</blockquote>
</i>Catching tigers in red weather - what a dream, what an illusion and imagination, though the only one enjoying it is a drunk sailor.<br /><br />Lucien Chardon is the drunk sailor of Balzac's <i>Lost Illusions</i> and offers the counterargument to Steven's cry for the imagination. Like Macbeth, Lucien's imagination is proleptic, always anticipatory of his greatness and his fame, and so he does not see the precarious path he walks on in the pursuit of those dreams. Within the dream of his success, he mortgages reality, he mortgages all he has and all he imagines he will have in order to reach his goal: <i>
  <blockquote>Not listening this time to the voice, he put his twelve hundred francs on the black and lost. He then felt within him that delicious sensation which succeeds the dreadful agitations of gamblers when, having nothing more to lose, they leave the flaming palace of their spasmodic dream.</blockquote>
</i>Lucien moves constantly from one oneiric palace-in-the-sky to the next, burning each to the ground, but shuffling quickly cloud-to-cloud to always keep himself afloat. But the real tragedy, and this book really approaches tragedy, is that Lucien's imagination sacrifices everyone whom he loves (David, his sister, his mother, Coralie), but never himself. Lucien seems impervious to his fall. And he does fall, and his name is the appropriate echo of the fallen angel Lucifer, for whom he is emblematic.<br /><br />Lucien is charismatic, he is naive, he knows himself to be a good poet but his illusions which shroud the reality of Paris life make his publishing success an impossibility. Throughout Balzac's Paris are heavy social commentaries on the print business for literature and poetry, a field with which he clearly had some familiarity. If Lucien is the fallen Lucifer, Literature and Art are God, but Journalists are the devils of Hell, which destroy art for sport. Lucien, more like Milton's Satan than the Bible's, is morally vague, he is a shadow of his Biblical predecessor, a parody of him even. In trying to good, Lucien invariable does bad. Like Macbeth, his crimes beget crimes, he makes matters worse, he is caught in the maelstrom of malefactions:<i>
  <blockquote>"It is written," cried Madame Chardon, "that my poor son is fated to do evil, as he said he was, even in doing good."</blockquote>
</i>While it seems easy to say 'Lucien's heart is in the right place' it would be to over value his morality. Lucien is a misguided Machiavellian. He is a Machiavellian in spirit, but only in his imagination is he capable of doing the means which would justify his ends; for Lucian is really very timid, clever and maybe talented but too timid to stick to one dogma or path to success.<br /><br />Lucien's illusions are literally invested in an identity: Lucien de Rubempr√©, an illustrious name which he adopts without having truly the right to bear it (though is related to the Rubempr√© line through his mother). It is this identity, this illusion, which is bold, which is brilliant, which is famous and beloved, and like a caterpillar turning to a painted butterfly, he hopes to shed his Chardon skin and leave his mother and sister and David behind him in Angoul√™me ('It is better to serve in Paris than rule in Angoul√™me!' I can almost hear him say). But in his attempt to transform himself he loses himself, he tries to jump over the gestation-chrysalidic phase, and in doing so cuts himself from all supports, headed for a free-fall which financially destroys David Sechard and his wife, Lucien's sister.<br /><br />The first section of <i>Lost Illusions</i> is entitled "The Two Poets" - which are Lucien and David. While Lucien represents a "true" poet in the definitive sense, David is a poet of science, in that he labors in his art to discover the truth. While the story of Lucien's fall is morbidly fascinating, and evokes pity and condemnation, the tragedy of David is heartbreaking and sympathetic. Ruined by his friend and brother-in-law, David starves as he works to revolutionize the printing process, and create a new, cheap stock of paper. David is a good man, a truly good man and loving husband, a virtuous man and a generous man. Son to a penny-pinching mogul who stole his wife's inheritance from his own son, David's story is an tenuously uplifting story of the virtuous struggle, which parallels Lucien's struggle to fame and downward race to the ethical bottom.<br /><br />Lucien is never fully removed of his illusions, even in the end, despite his regret for the fate which befalls David, a new dream quickly replaces the one that has died and fallen away. What can can be said of Lucien's imagination is that it is phoenix-like in its endurance. So why is it called <i>Lost Illusions</i>? I think to Steven's poem again, disillusionment in routine, in the middling repetition of the bourgeois. Despite his success in invention, David never financially enjoys his success, but rather sells his enterprise to feed his family. His struggle has unchained him from the illusion. We feel his inventions are done, he is "happy" - his ending is a happy one, but a reservedly happy one. We want David to succeed, to achieve the greatness which he deserves and which Lucien seeks. But he doesn't get that, he gets a complaisance, he gets by, and he and Eve are OK with that. He doesn't foster the illusion of success or of fame, nor of wealth nor ambition of any kind. His house is haunted by white night-gowns, none of them are "strange with socks of lace and beaded scintures." He will live happily, we believe that, but he will live only a half-life, and that wounds us.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6802106587026836986" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_978799973" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/978799973" />
    <a title="Henry Martin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14734360-henry-martin"><img alt="Henry Martin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442649786p2/14734360.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/978799973?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Henry Martin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Henry Martin" href="/user/show/14734360-henry-martin">Henry Martin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14734360-henry-martin?shelf=permanent-collection">permanent-collection</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer978799973" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7160630593741645859">Balzac's Lost Illusions is a massive literary undertaking, and an attempt to delve deep into the world of humanity with all its great deeds and basest desires. Yet, taking the entire volume of Balzac's Human Comedy into perspective, Lost Illusions is nothing but a small piece of the enormous mosaic this author created in the short span of a decade. <br/><br/>Like with all his works I read to date, Lost Illusions offers its readers spectacular writing, well developed characters, just enough but not too muc</span>
  <span id="freeText7160630593741645859" style="display:none">Balzac's Lost Illusions is a massive literary undertaking, and an attempt to delve deep into the world of humanity with all its great deeds and basest desires. Yet, taking the entire volume of Balzac's Human Comedy into perspective, Lost Illusions is nothing but a small piece of the enormous mosaic this author created in the short span of a decade. <br /><br />Like with all his works I read to date, Lost Illusions offers its readers spectacular writing, well developed characters, just enough but not too much backstory, and a purely human conflict with multitude of players affecting the final outcome. <br /><br />While most of the works in the Human Comedy take place in Paris, Lost illusions offers a glimpse of the life in the countryside; nevertheless, the ambitions there are not much different, the nobles are just as bad (if not worse) than their Parisian counterparts, and the long-reaching allure of the Parisian society finds a fertile ground amidst the country nobles. <br /><br />The story opens quite simply with an old man, a printing press, and a child in the beautiful French countryside. The child goes to study the art of printing in Paris, the father sees him as his successor, and there is the making of a bright future, of growing business, of independence, and a happy life. <br /><br />Readers familiar only with contemporary genre works will likely expect a happy-ever-after, and probably wonder why there are five hundred pages yet to be read in the book. Ha, they do not know Balzac. <br /><br />In a few pages, the printing press turns out to be an aging building with antiquated technology, the father shows his darker side and his avarice, and the son, while educated and humble, lacks any balls whatsoever. <br /><br />As the pages turn, more and more characters make their appearance, some nice, some mean, and some downright ugly. The list of main players quickly grows to more than a few, and the plot thickens. <br /><br />Without disclosing any of the plot (and there are several plots running at once), I must bow and show my respect to M. Balzac. Lost Illusions is one heck of a novel, and one heck of a study of humanity, at its best and at its worst. Balzac, as expected, throws some unexpected punches, stirs some unforeseen troubles, and lets you get down to the muck and get dirty while you are at it. He knows humanity, he knows what makes us tick, and he knows how to shine the light from just the right angle. <br /><br />Bravo, sir!<br /><br />That being said . . . there are those who connect with Lucien and disregard David. I could not. Lucien is his own character, and yes, he plays a large part in this story. David, however, David is the story. I can relate to David better than I can relate to any character in this work.<br /><br />I'm still a bot torn between Pere Goriot and Lost Illusions. If I had to make a choice, I would not know which one I liked better. The two works are very different, and yet very similar at the same time. Both books are on my 'favorites' list. <br /><br />One final note - some reviews mentioned how different Rastignac was in this novel from how he was portrayed in Pere Goriot. Balzac has some two thousand characters circulating throughout his work, and making appearances here and there, sometimes playing a major, yet other time a very minor, part. Balzac's narrators are describing the characters, and each narrator sees a person differently. Also, we must keep in mind the transformation Rastignac underwent following Goriot's funeral, and the last lines of the novel. <br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7160630593741645859" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Jonfaith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2327886-jonfaith"><img alt="Jonfaith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534772484p2/2327886.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer141936267" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11075213050151180429">While there were issues with the structure of the novel, the disparate scenarios involving Lucien and David are removed from one another to a cumbersome degree. Compounding this, the tragedy which envelops David and Eve is soaked and blurred in jargon and legal asides. I sense that Balzac was thinking long-term and indifferent to these quibbles. That said, Lost Illusions is a narrative triumph and one i will treasure.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2165822063" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2165822063" />
    <a title="Gabrielle Dubois" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28233498-gabrielle-dubois"><img alt="Gabrielle Dubois" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510402879p2/28233498.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2165822063?book_show_action=true">Oct 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gabrielle Dubois" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gabrielle Dubois" href="/user/show/28233498-gabrielle-dubois">Gabrielle Dubois</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28233498-gabrielle-dubois?shelf=19th-century">19th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2165822063" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9808352894430202982">Balzac said about his Lost Illusions that they were "the main work in the work". It's a novel about the functioning of the "literature machine".<br/>Lucien, a young poet from Angoul√™me, local but not very big fame, is taken to Paris by his protector Mme de Bargenton. In Paris, all the illusions are allowed to him, which doesn‚Äôt mean that they will necessarily become realities.<br/><br/>Balzac, work and money.<br/>To make a fortune was the eternal thought of Balzac. He ran after gold and money while creditors and ba</span>
  <span id="freeText9808352894430202982" style="display:none">Balzac said about his Lost Illusions that they were "the main work in the work". It's a novel about the functioning of the "literature machine".<br />Lucien, a young poet from Angoul√™me, local but not very big fame, is taken to Paris by his protector Mme de Bargenton. In Paris, all the illusions are allowed to him, which doesn‚Äôt mean that they will necessarily become realities.<br /><br />Balzac, work and money.<br />To make a fortune was the eternal thought of Balzac. He ran after gold and money while creditors and bailiffs ran after him and Balzac hid from them to work like a Vulcan on his forge.<br />Excerpt from the Illusions: "My God! my kingdom for gold!" said Lucien to himself, "gold is the only power in front of which everyone kneels." No! his conscience shouted to him, "Glory, is the power, and glory is work! Work! In launching his enraged words, he dined poorly at Hurbain's restaurant."<br /><br />Balzac and the food.<br />Big coffee drinker, Balzac didn‚Äôt always have enough to feed himself or feed those he invited to dine at home. So sometimes Balzac fed on illusions, and it was up to his guests to do the same; but they didn‚Äôt regret it because a conversation with Balzac fed their brains more than a thousand meals would have fed their stomachs!<br />Excerpt from the Illusions: "There are few students housed in the Quartier Latin who haven‚Äôt eaten in Flicoteaux's restaurant, this temple of hunger and misery. What, no doubt, prevented Flicoteaux, this friend of the youth, from making a colossal fortune, is an article he had printed in large letters: BREAD AT DISCRETION, that is to say, until indiscretion. Many glories had Flicoteaux for foster father."<br /><br />Balzac and fashion.<br />Balzac had a taste or didn‚Äôt have any... But for a long time he appeared in Paris wearing a singular blue suit which his astonished and amused contemporaries remembered in their memoirs. Because of the lack of money, of the sake of wearing out his costumes or for a personal taste and comfort, Balzac opted for a simple cream linen dress looking like a monk‚Äôs suit, in which he had himself painted. But as the suit doesn‚Äôt make the writer, his didn‚Äôt prevent him from producing a masterly work.<br />Excerpt from the Illusions: "After the sad test of the elegant life which had absorbed all its capital, Lucien threw himself into work with that first ardor that dispels so quickly the difficulties and the amusements that Paris offers to all the existences, the richest, like the poorest, and who, to be tamed, demand the savagery of true talent or the bleak desire of ambition."<br /><br />Balzac and the publishers.<br />Excerpt from a letter written to a friend by Balzac when he was twenty-four: "For my novel Wann-Chlore, they offer me, guess what? 600 francs! I would rather go to plow the earth with my nails than to consent to such an infamy."<br />Excerpt from the Illusions: Mr. Doguereau was an old school bookseller, a man of the time when the booksellers wanted to hold locked in an attic Voltaire and Montesquieu dying of hunger. After reading Lucien's manuscript, the librarian wants to publish it. "Doguereau came to the hotel where Lucien lived. He was determined to pay one thousand francs... Seeing the hotel, the old fox changed gradually his mind. A young man living there has only modest tastes, I can give him only eight hundred francs. Lucien is a handsome boy, if he earned too much money he would have fun with it, he wouldn‚Äôt work anymore. In our common interest, I will offer him six hundred francs..."<br /><br />In The Lost Illusions, we find Balzac's detailed descriptions of a provincial town, its local bourgeoisie, traders and entrepreneurs. Balzac also paints us the Parisian fauna: noble and rich people, booksellers, journalists, mondains, poor poets, a youth hungry for earthly, intellectual and makeshift food. The author puts himself in the spirit of each of his characters and we sit with Balzac in a provincial salon, we walk with him on a Parisian sidewalk, we dine with him in a poor restaurant, we feel with him the humiliation of being poor in Paris when we were rich in the provinces.<br />√î how I understand the Parisian part of The Lost Illusions! "Unfortunately, it‚Äôs necessary to show off before to be," said Theophile Gautier, and the Lucien by Balzac feels cruelly this quote!<br />The big question asked by Balzac is: does literature have to have a price, and if yes, what price?<br />You think I told you everything about The Lost Illusions? Far from it! But it's so exhausting to make these comments in English... but I like to kill myself with work!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9808352894430202982" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_246181303" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/246181303" />
    <a title="Elizabeth (Alaska)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2026178-elizabeth-alaska"><img alt="Elizabeth (Alaska)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304966095p2/2026178.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/246181303?book_show_action=true">Dec 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth (Alaska)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth (Alaska)" href="/user/show/2026178-elizabeth-alaska">Elizabeth (Alaska)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=nineteenth-century">nineteenth-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=author-balzac">author-balzac</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer246181303" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12276137227936981538">This is a trilogy, consisting of:<br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9706944.Two_Poets__Dodo_Press_' title='Two Poets (Dodo Press) by Honor√© de Balzac' rel='nofollow noopener'>Two Poets</a><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2366618.A_Distinguished_Provincial_at_Paris__Dodo_Press_' title='A Distinguished Provincial at Paris (Dodo Press) by Honor√© de Balzac' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Distinguished Provincial at Paris</a> and<br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5601598.Eve_and_David' title='Eve and David by Honor√© de Balzac' rel='nofollow noopener'>Eve and David</a><br/><br/>Originally published separately in 1837, 1839, and 1843, they are now usually combined in one volume under the title of Lost Illusions. However, if you find them separately, be sure to read them in that order.<br/><br/>This starts very slowly. Had I not read other Balzac, I might not have continued past the first 40-50 pages. Soon, however, the story begins to reveal itself, and I could not help myself. There are</span>
  <span id="freeText12276137227936981538" style="display:none">This is a trilogy, consisting of:<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9706944.Two_Poets__Dodo_Press_" title="Two Poets (Dodo Press) by Honor√© de Balzac" rel="nofollow noopener">Two Poets</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2366618.A_Distinguished_Provincial_at_Paris__Dodo_Press_" title="A Distinguished Provincial at Paris (Dodo Press) by Honor√© de Balzac" rel="nofollow noopener">A Distinguished Provincial at Paris</a> and<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5601598.Eve_and_David" title="Eve and David by Honor√© de Balzac" rel="nofollow noopener">Eve and David</a><br /><br />Originally published separately in 1837, 1839, and 1843, they are now usually combined in one volume under the title of Lost Illusions. However, if you find them separately, be sure to read them in that order.<br /><br />This starts very slowly. Had I not read other Balzac, I might not have continued past the first 40-50 pages. Soon, however, the story begins to reveal itself, and I could not help myself. There are further slow places. The longer middle section of Balzac's story is of Paris some 20 years earlier than its publication and provides valuable background of a Paris in the Restoration. While not riveting, perhaps, it is a history of journalism and the publishing industry. I suspect this is the primary reason the trilogy makes it to the 1001 list.<br /><br />Unlike Dickens, whose characters are either good or bad, you will not find an unflawed character in Balzac. In Lost Illusions, you will be hard-pressed to find an unselfish one. Not only is everyone out for their own self-interests, but, with few exceptions, each is compelled to succeed by trampling another - sometimes as many of the others as possible. Sometimes I felt my head spinning over so much double-dealing!<br /><br />I do like Balzac. My 4 stars might not be your 4 stars, and I admit this just barely makes into that grouping for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12276137227936981538" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Dagny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10742602-dagny"><img alt="Dagny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1341610852p2/10742602.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dagny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dagny" href="/user/show/10742602-dagny">Dagny</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780375757907" href="/book/show/702445.Lost_Illusions">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10742602-dagny?shelf=balzac-honore-de">balzac-honore-de</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10742602-dagny?shelf=french">french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10742602-dagny?shelf=19th-century">19th-century</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer363123307" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3460628920103969903">Lost Illusions (Illusions Perdues) is a trilogy of three novels which should be read in order:<br/>The Two Poets (Les Deux Poetes)<br/>A Distinguished Provincial at Paris/A Great Man of the Provinces in Paris (Un Grand homme de province a Paris)<br/>Eve and David/The Trials of the Inventor (Le Souffrances de l'inventeur)<br/><br/>The story continues in a fourth novel:<br/>Scenes from a Courtesan's Life/A Harlot High and Low (Splendeurs et Miseres des Courtisanes)<br/><br/>See the individual titles for more information.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2280266720" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2280266720" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/442654-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513093267p2/442654.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/442654-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2280266720" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11757815805933823310">It's a long haul, for sure, and it's very much a 19th Century novel, but if you're up for a 700-page book about a provincial youth corrupted by the evils of city life, it's worth it -- especially in the middle sections, as Lucien is taken in by the world of Paris, and the weird dialogue with the Spanish priest towards the end. Anyone who has ever written for a living has a little Lucien in them -- we all make our pacts with the devil, whether that devil is a corrupt official or a copywriting gig</span>
  <span id="freeText11757815805933823310" style="display:none">It's a long haul, for sure, and it's very much a 19th Century novel, but if you're up for a 700-page book about a provincial youth corrupted by the evils of city life, it's worth it -- especially in the middle sections, as Lucien is taken in by the world of Paris, and the weird dialogue with the Spanish priest towards the end. Anyone who has ever written for a living has a little Lucien in them -- we all make our pacts with the devil, whether that devil is a corrupt official or a copywriting gig you secretly loathe.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('3UBe217lnpYuDL3VEw/mLnSY7SZdtQsHXcd0s+BZCXM8rlpia/mfUdEBIpUjiGwY+4jfuuw9R/lC0oeZbakImA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8T5fcjw5gGZa3ps9MTgzyeMGe8k+qQ291vj8a19evr0Q0FvLCSWBoaXTBH0Bv7n/bBZJVY8hQUPJ7Q9B0q6/Vg==')})
  }

During Zola\'s lifetime it was the bestselling of all his novels, praised by contemporaries for its epic sweep as well as for its attention to historical detail. La Debacle seeks to explain why the Second Empire ended in a crushing military defeat and revolutionary violence. It focuses on ordinary soldiers, showing their bravery and suffering in the midst of circumstances they cannot control, and includes some of the most powerful description Zola ever wrote. Zola skilfully integrates his narrative of events and the fictional lives of his characters to provide the finest account of this tragic chapter in the history of France. Young, attractive and very ambitious, George Duroy, known to his admirers as Bel-Ami, is offered a job as a journalist on La Vie francaise and soon makes a great success of his new career. But he also comes face to face with the realities of the corrupt society in which he lives - the sleazy colleagues, the manipulative mistresses, and wily financiers - and swiftly learns to become an arch-seducer, blackmailer and social climber in a world where love is only a means to an end. Written when Maupassant was at the height of his powers, &quot;Bel-Ami&quot; is a novel of great frankness and cynicism, but it is also infused with the sheer joy of life - depicting the scenes and characters of Paris in the belle epoque with wit, sensitivity, and humanity. Douglas Parmee\'s translation captures all the vigour and vitality of Maupassant\'s novel. His introduction explores the similarities between Bel-Ami and Maupassant himself and demonstrates the skill with which the author depicts his large cast of characters and the French society of the       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/228089.Honor_de_Balzac">About Honor√© de Balzac</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/228089.Honor_de_Balzac"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1206567834p3/228089.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7203626506411960994">Honor√© de Balzac was a nineteenth-century French novelist and playwright. His magnum opus was a sequence of almost 100 novels and plays collectively entitled La Com√©die humaine, which presents a panorama of French life in the years after the fall of Napol√©on Bonaparte in 1815.<br/><br/>Due to his keen observation of fine detail and unfiltered representation of society, Balzac is regarded as one of the found</span>
  <span id="freeText7203626506411960994" style="display:none">Honor√© de Balzac was a nineteenth-century French novelist and playwright. His magnum opus was a sequence of almost 100 novels and plays collectively entitled La Com√©die humaine, which presents a panorama of French life in the years after the fall of Napol√©on Bonaparte in 1815.<br /><br />Due to his keen observation of fine detail and unfiltered representation of society, Balzac is regarded as one of the founders of realism in European literature. He is renowned for his multi-faceted characters; even his lesser characters are complex, morally ambiguous and fully human. Inanimate objects are imbued with character as well; the city of Paris, a backdrop for much of his writing, takes on many human qualities. His writing influenced many famous authors, including the novelists Marcel Proust, √âmile Zola, Charles Dickens, Gustave Flaubert, Henry James and Jack Kerouac, as well as important philosophers such as Friedrich Engels. Many of Balzac's works have been made into films, and they continue to inspire other writers.<br /><br />An enthusiastic reader and independent thinker as a child, Balzac had trouble adapting himself to the teaching style of his grammar school. His willful nature caused trouble throughout his life, and frustrated his ambitions to succeed in the world of business. When he finished school, Balzac was apprenticed as a legal clerk, but he turned his back on law after wearying of its inhumanity and banal routine. Before and during his career as a writer, he attempted to be a publisher, printer, businessman, critic, and politician. He failed in all of these efforts. La Com√©die Humaine reflects his real-life difficulties, and includes scenes from his own experience.<br /><br />Balzac suffered from health problems throughout his life, possibly due to his intense writing schedule. His relationship with his family was often strained by financial and personal drama, and he lost more than one friend over critical reviews. In 1850, he married Ewelina Ha≈Ñska, his longtime paramour; he passed away five months later.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7203626506411960994" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
